                </ul>
            </div>
        </div>
    </div>
</section>

<div id="anp_a" class="d-panel p" style="display: none"><div class="notes p"><section id="historyarticle-meta1" class="history"><p>Received 2020 Dec 29; Accepted 2021 Jan 14; Collection date 2021 Jan.</p></section></div></div>

This article is an open access article distributed under the terms and conditions of the Creative Commons Attribution (CC BY) license (<a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/</a>).</p>

</div>
</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="abstract1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p>The recent legalization of <em>Cannabis sativa</em> L. in many regions has revealed a need for effective propagation and biotechnologies for the species. Micropropagation affords researchers and producers methods to rapidly propagate insect-/disease-/virus-free clonal plants and store germplasm and forms the basis for other biotechnologies. Despite this need, research in the area is limited due to the long history of prohibitions and restrictions. Existing literature has multiple limitations: many publications use hemp as a proxy for drug-type <em>Cannabis</em> when it is well established that there is significant genotype specificity; studies using drug-type cultivars are predominantly optimized using a single cultivar; most protocols have not been replicated by independent groups, and some attempts demonstrate a lack of reproducibility across genotypes. Due to culture decline and other problems, the multiplication phase of micropropagation (Stage 2) has not been fully developed in many reports. This review will provide a brief background on the history and botany of <em>Cannabis</em> as well as a comprehensive and critical summary of <em>Cannabis</em> tissue culture. Special attention will be paid to current challenges faced by researchers, the limitations of existing <em>Cannabis</em> micropropagation studies, and recent developments and future directions of <em>Cannabis</em> tissue culture technologies. </p>
<section id="kwd-group1" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Keywords:</strong> <em>Cannabis</em>, marijuana, marihuana, tissue culture, review, regeneration, floral reversion, micropropagation, TDZ, DKW</p></section></section><section id="sec1-plants-10-00185"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">1. Introduction</h2>
<p><em>Cannabis sativa</em> L. is rising to prominence as a commercial crop for industrial, food, medical, and recreational applications. The current wave of interest has been characterized by a growing number of countries easing restrictions around research, commercial cultivation, and sale of dried <em>Cannabis</em> flowers, extracts, and consumable, medicinal, or industrial products. With interest renewed in this crop, which has been cultivated for thousands of years, research and innovation in the coming decades is expected to deepen our understanding of the growth, physiology, and biochemistry of <em>C. sativa.</em> Our improved understanding of this important plant will enable large-scale micropropagation, genetic preservation, and the development of plant biotechnologies for advanced new plant breeding technologies (NPBTs). The application of plant biotechnologies and NPBTs will require effective, high-throughput In Vitro culture systems that will allow for transformation and subsequent scale-up of any novel cultivars developed by micropropagation. Once developed, an effective transformation system will require regeneration and clonal propagation systems that can be reliably replicated in multiple lab environments and that can effectively be scaled up to meet the commercial industry’s needs. To build these robust regeneration and micropropagation systems, methods must be tested across multiple drug and fiber-type genotypes, be flexible with the age and condition of plant tissues, and be able to accommodate small differences in culture conditions that will inevitably arise from different lab environments and from the transfer to a large-scale commercial tissue culture operation. This review offers a critical analysis of the existing published and pre-print <em>C. sativa</em> micropropagation and regeneration literature and highlights the current shortfalls to help direct current and future research to catch up on decades of lost opportunities due to the criminalization and overregulation of <em>Cannabis.</em>
</p></section><section id="sec2-plants-10-00185"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">2. Brief History of <em>C. sativa</em> in North America</h2>
<p>The relevance of <em>Cannabis</em> as a versatile crop for oilseed, fiber, medicinal, and recreational drug production spans millennia. Between 1000 and 2000 BCE, <em>Cannabis</em> was introduced to Western Asia, Europe, and Egypt as a fiber crop for producing cloth, ship ropes, and paper. After 500 CE, the cultivation of <em>Cannabis</em> was widespread across Europe [<a href="#B1-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-plants-10-00185">1</a>,<a href="#B2-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-plants-10-00185">2</a>]; however, it was not until 1545 and 1606 that it was introduced to South and North America, respectively [<a href="#B3-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-plants-10-00185">3</a>]. Despite its centuries-long cultivation, the beginning of the 20th century saw its recreational use outlawed and medicinal use strongly curtailed by an addendum to the League of Nations’ 1912 Opium Convention. This act pushed countries around the globe to restrict and criminalize <em>Cannabis</em> [<a href="#B4-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B4-plants-10-00185">4</a>]. </p>
<p>In Canada, <em>Cannabis</em> was made illegal following its addition to the Opium and Drug Act in 1923 [<a href="#B5-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B5-plants-10-00185">5</a>,<a href="#B6-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-plants-10-00185">6</a>], and the United States followed suit with the 1937 Marijuana Tax Act, severely restricting the medicinal use of <em>Cannabis</em> in the United States [<a href="#B6-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-plants-10-00185">6</a>,<a href="#B7-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B7-plants-10-00185">7</a>]. <em>Cannabis</em> had been included in the United States Pharmacopoeia since 1850 and was removed in 1942, a few years after passage of the Marijuana Tax Act of 1937 [<a href="#B8-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B8-plants-10-00185">8</a>]. In the United States, <em>Cannabis</em> is classified under the most restrictive drug class (Schedule I) as part of the Comprehensive Drug Abuse Prevention and Control Act of 1970. This 1970 act overturned the 1937 Marijuana Tax Act and states that <em>Cannabis</em> has “no apparent medical potential and a high likelihood of abuse” [<a href="#B9-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-plants-10-00185">9</a>,<a href="#B10-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-plants-10-00185">10</a>]. These restrictions, which made no distinction between fibrous hemp and drug-type <em>Cannabis,</em> had the unfortunate consequence of limiting most <em>Cannabis</em> research by making its acquisition for research purposes challenging [<a href="#B8-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B8-plants-10-00185">8</a>,<a href="#B9-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-plants-10-00185">9</a>]. Commercial production of industrial hemp (<em>C. sativa</em> with &lt;0.3% Δ<sup>9</sup>-tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) by dry weight [<a href="#B11-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B11-plants-10-00185">11</a>]) has faced many of the same restrictions as drug-type (&gt;0.3% THC by dry weight) <em>Cannabis</em> in North America, as the distinction between the two has been largely ignored by government and law enforcement [<a href="#B9-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-plants-10-00185">9</a>]. </p>
<p>The strict conditions that regulate <em>Cannabis</em> research have created challenges throughout the research pipeline [<a href="#B10-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-plants-10-00185">10</a>,<a href="#B12-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B12-plants-10-00185">12</a>]. Early small-scale clinical trials have investigated the use of cannabinoids to treat comorbidities of autism spectrum disorder, anxiety, chronic pain, and seizures and have shown promising results, but research in this area has been highly restricted and progress has been slow [<a href="#B13-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B13-plants-10-00185">13</a>,<a href="#B14-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-plants-10-00185">14</a>,<a href="#B15-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B15-plants-10-00185">15</a>,<a href="#B16-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-plants-10-00185">16</a>,<a href="#B17-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B17-plants-10-00185">17</a>]. Likewise, these restrictions and the lack of a legal industry have limited research on agronomic, horticultural, and biotechnological aspects of the crop. As a result, relative to the economic importance, technological development is in its infancy and many techniques that are routine for most species are not developed in <em>Cannabis</em>.</p>
<p>In recent years, this has started to change as countries around the world have started to lift some restrictions. In Canada, commercial production of hemp was legalized in 1998 [<a href="#B9-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-plants-10-00185">9</a>]; however, regulatory barriers and a lack of market interest resulted in a very slow-growing industry until recently [<a href="#B18-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18-plants-10-00185">18</a>]. In the United States, a pilot-scale production of industrial hemp was legalized in 2014 followed by commercial-scale federal legalization in the 2018 farm bill [<a href="#B9-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-plants-10-00185">9</a>,<a href="#B18-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18-plants-10-00185">18</a>]. Prior to this change, federally funded research in the US could only be conducted with <em>Cannabis</em> obtained from the National Institute on Drug Abuse (NIDA). With the passing of the 2018 farm bill, hemp can now be used for research, but drug-type <em>Cannabis</em> is still highly restricted at the federal level. In 2013, the Marihuana for Medical Purposes Regulations were implemented by the Government of Canada, laying the groundwork for commercial production of medicinal <em>Cannabis</em> [<a href="#B8-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B8-plants-10-00185">8</a>,<a href="#B19-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B19-plants-10-00185">19</a>]. The legalization of the possession, growth, and consumption of <em>Cannabis</em> for recreational purposes followed in October 2018. At the international level, regulations are also beginning to change; a landmark decision by the United Nations Commission on Narcotic Drugs (CND) voted to remove <em>Cannabis</em> from Schedule IV of the 1961 Single Convention on Narcotic Drugs in December 2020, thereby recognizing the medicinal and therapeutic uses of <em>Cannabis</em> [<a href="#B20-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B20-plants-10-00185">20</a>]. While still highly regulated, the legalization of <em>Cannabis</em> for medical and recreational consumption in Canada, the legalization of hemp in the United States, and a similar trend around the world has resulted in a renaissance period for <em>Cannabis</em> research.</p></section><section id="sec3-plants-10-00185"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">3. Botany and Taxonomy of <em>C. sativa</em>
</h2>
<p><em>Cannabis sativa</em> L. (<em>Cannabis</em>, hemp, marijuana) is an annual flowering plant of the family Cannabaceae. Although <em>Cannabis</em> is usually dioecious, hermaphroditism occurs in some cultivars (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>A) and both formal and informal breeding programs have resulted in some monoecious cultivars, primarily restricted to hemp [<a href="#B21-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21-plants-10-00185">21</a>,<a href="#B22-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B22-plants-10-00185">22</a>,<a href="#B23-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23-plants-10-00185">23</a>]. The family Cannabaceae consists of ten genera, containing over 100 accepted species, with <em>Humulus lupulus</em> L. (hops; the chief ingredient in beer) being a notable member [<a href="#B24-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24-plants-10-00185">24</a>,<a href="#B25-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25-plants-10-00185">25</a>]<em>. C. sativa</em> is native to central Asia, likely in the foothills of the Himalayan Mountain Range [<a href="#B1-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-plants-10-00185">1</a>,<a href="#B2-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-plants-10-00185">2</a>]. <em>Cannabis</em> is a fast-growing plant, growing up to 10 cm a day and reaching heights of 6 m in its native habitat, while growth in temperate climates is usually lower [<a href="#B23-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23-plants-10-00185">23</a>,<a href="#B26-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B26-plants-10-00185">26</a>,<a href="#B27-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B27-plants-10-00185">27</a>].</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-10-00185-f001"><h3 class="obj_head">Figure 1.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7835777_plants-10-00185-g001.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fc6c/7835777/a2123dce17d7/plants-10-00185-g001.jpg" loading="lazy" height="502" width="742" alt="Figure 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-10-00185-f001/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>In Vitro flowering of <em>Cannabis sativa.</em> (<strong>A</strong>) Flowering <em>C. sativa</em> male plant displaying a hermaphroditic phenotype, showing female flowers (left) adjacent to male flowers (right). Scale bar—1 mm. (<strong>B</strong>) In Vitro male inflorescences of <em>C. sativa</em>. Scale bar—1 mm (<strong>C</strong>) A pair of female <em>C. sativa</em> florets obtained from In Vitro flowering <em>C. sativa</em>. Scale bar—1 mm. (<strong>D</strong>) Glandular trichomes developing on the bract surrounding the ovary of a female <em>C. sativa</em> inflorescence. Scale bar—2 mm. (<strong>E</strong>) Mature flowering In Vitro explant of <em>C. sativa.</em> Scale bar—1 cm. (<strong>F</strong>) Four-week-old vegetative explants reverted from In Vitro <em>C. sativa</em> inflorescences. Scale bar—1 cm.</p></figcaption></figure><p>When grown from a seed, the first true leaves are pairs of oppositely oriented single leaflets (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>A). As the plant matures, the phyllotaxy shifts from opposite to alternate leaf arrangement and the number of leaflets per leaf increases (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>B; Clarke 1999; Spitzer-Rimon et al. 2019). Leaves on a mature plant are digitate with anywhere from 5 to 11 leaflets and have a long petiole, although during flowering, they often revert to producing lower numbers of leaflets [<a href="#B23-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23-plants-10-00185">23</a>,<a href="#B28-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-plants-10-00185">28</a>]. <em>Cannabis</em> is predominantly a short-day plant, with flowering induced by 12- to 14-h photoperiods [<a href="#B29-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-plants-10-00185">29</a>]; however, some photoperiod-insensitive cultivars have been developed. Male and female plants cannot easily be distinguished until flowers begin to appear [<a href="#B26-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B26-plants-10-00185">26</a>,<a href="#B30-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-plants-10-00185">30</a>]. Male flowers have five green or yellow petals and are larger than female flowers (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>B). Female flowers consist of an ovule enclosed in a thin green bract with two yellow/whiteish stigma emerging from the closed bracts (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>C) [<a href="#B26-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B26-plants-10-00185">26</a>,<a href="#B30-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-plants-10-00185">30</a>]. During the development of the flower, before the elongation of the stigma, glandular trichomes develop on the bract surrounding the ovary (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>D) [<a href="#B22-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B22-plants-10-00185">22</a>]. Two main types of trichomes can be found covering <em>Cannabis</em> plants: glandular and non-glandular trichomes. Only the former produce cannabinoids in any considerable quantity, and glandular trichomes are predominantly found on the bracts and floral leaves of female plants (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>D). Male plants produce few, if any, glandular trichomes [<a href="#B28-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-plants-10-00185">28</a>,<a href="#B31-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B31-plants-10-00185">31</a>]. Due to their low levels of cannabinoids, male plants are generally not consumed as a medicinal or recreational drug and will not be extensively discussed in this review.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-10-00185-f002"><h3 class="obj_head">Figure 2.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7835777_plants-10-00185-g002.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fc6c/7835777/6583daafa2d1/plants-10-00185-g002.jpg" loading="lazy" height="501" width="746" alt="Figure 2"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-10-00185-f002/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>(<strong>A</strong>) In Vitro germinated seedling of <em>C. sativa</em> demonstrating opposite leaf arrangement. White arrows show oppositely oriented first true leaves. Scale bar—1 cm. (<strong>B</strong>) A Stage 2 vegetative explant (subcultured from a nodal explant) of <em>C. sativa</em> demonstrating alternate leaf arrangement (black arrows), a change in phyllotaxy resulting from explant maturation. Scale bar—1 cm. (<strong>C</strong>) <em>C. sativa</em> grown in controlled environment growth chambers under fluorescent lighting. (<strong>D</strong>) <em>C. sativa</em> grown outdoors under a shade cloth in Colombia. Image supplied courtesy of Avicanna™. (<strong>E</strong>) Hyperhydric <em>C. sativa</em> explants growing on Murashige and Skoog (MS) medium supplemented with 0.5 µM thidiazuron (TDZ).</p></figcaption></figure><p>The taxonomy of the genus <em>Cannabis</em> is a matter of spirited debate and no consensus has emerged on whether it is a monospecific or polyspecific genus [<a href="#B32-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B32-plants-10-00185">32</a>,<a href="#B33-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B33-plants-10-00185">33</a>,<a href="#B34-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34-plants-10-00185">34</a>]. The ability to distinguish between hemp and drug-type <em>Cannabis</em> has been the subject of much interest by law enforcement, which relies on THC content for distinction [<a href="#B35-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B35-plants-10-00185">35</a>]. From a law enforcement and regulatory standpoint, the two main categories of <em>Cannabis</em> have been described as “drug-type” (medicinal or recreational) and “fiber-type” (industrial hemp), the drug-type generally being dioecious, with a short, wide, bush-like growth pattern, while the fiber-type can be either dioecious or monoecious with a tall and thin growth pattern [<a href="#B36-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B36-plants-10-00185">36</a>]. However, this distinction is further complicated by hemp developed for seed or non-psychoactive cannabinoids, which often morphologically resemble drug-type <em>Cannabis</em>. Two distinct <em>Cannabis</em> chemotypes have been identified, which also fall in line with the two aforementioned morphological groups and are largely defined by their THC content. The fiber-type <em>Cannabis</em>, or “hemp”, has a THC dry weight in the flowering heads of &lt;0.3% or &lt;0.2% depending on the jurisdiction [<a href="#B9-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-plants-10-00185">9</a>,<a href="#B11-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B11-plants-10-00185">11</a>,<a href="#B18-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18-plants-10-00185">18</a>]. Hemp can often be accompanied by a higher cannabidiol (CBD) content (THC:CBD &lt; 1), while the elite drug-type cultivars typically report a THC:CBD ratio &gt;1, or &gt;0.3% THC in the flower heads [<a href="#B9-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-plants-10-00185">9</a>,<a href="#B37-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B37-plants-10-00185">37</a>].</p>
<p>However, a taxonomic system based on THC:CBD ratios has faced scrutiny [<a href="#B38-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B38-plants-10-00185">38</a>] and other classification systems that further divide the species based on chemotype have been suggested. These include classifications based on other secondary metabolites produced by the <em>Cannabis</em> rather than solely the THC and CBD levels [<a href="#B39-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B39-plants-10-00185">39</a>,<a href="#B40-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B40-plants-10-00185">40</a>]. Early genetic studies attempting to distinguish between the genetic fingerprints of hemp and <em>Cannabis</em> have suggested that the chief differentiation factor between the two plants was a single locus that determined the production of THC or CBD synthases [<a href="#B41-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B41-plants-10-00185">41</a>]. These findings have been echoed by whole genomic and transcriptomic assemblies of hemp and drug-type <em>Cannabis,</em> which have shown that hemp plants have high levels of cannabidiolic acid synthase <em>(CBDAS</em>) genes and transcripts, while the <em>THCAS</em> gene encoding the oxidocyclase enzyme, which forms tetrahydrocannabinolic acid (THCA), is dominant in drug-type cultivars [<a href="#B2-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-plants-10-00185">2</a>,<a href="#B42-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B42-plants-10-00185">42</a>]. However, recent work using single-nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) has shown that the genetic differences between hemp extend beyond the loci responsible for cannabinoid production and are instead found throughout the entire genome [<a href="#B35-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B35-plants-10-00185">35</a>].</p>
<p>Drug-type <em>Cannabis</em> has been historically described by enthusiasts as consisting of three species: <em>C. sativa</em>, <em>C. indica</em>, and <em>C. ruderalis</em>. The diversity of chemical and morphological traits within <em>Cannabis</em> has led some taxonomists to agree with this and propose that <em>Cannabis</em> should be considered a polyspecific genus containing multiple individual species: <em>sativa</em>, <em>indica</em>, and <em>ruderalis</em> [<a href="#B43-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B43-plants-10-00185">43</a>,<a href="#B44-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B44-plants-10-00185">44</a>,<a href="#B45-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B45-plants-10-00185">45</a>]. Further sub-speciation has even been suggested within these groups [<a href="#B34-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34-plants-10-00185">34</a>,<a href="#B46-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B46-plants-10-00185">46</a>]; however, this nomenclature has yet to be widely used. The taxonomy of drug-type <em>Cannabis</em> is complicated by years of prohibition, which resulted in informal, clandestine breeding programs that caused decades of interbreeding and hybridization without records of parentage [<a href="#B40-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B40-plants-10-00185">40</a>,<a href="#B46-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B46-plants-10-00185">46</a>]. The ability to consistently and reliably distinguish between <em>sativa</em> and <em>indica</em> types of <em>Cannabis</em> has been scrutinized [<a href="#B35-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B35-plants-10-00185">35</a>], and as a result of these underground breeding programs, establishing the pedigree of <em>Cannabis</em> is incredibly challenging and has resulted in unpredictability for consumers of <em>C. sativa</em> products [<a href="#B33-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B33-plants-10-00185">33</a>]. Concerns have also been raised that this ever-increasing introgression is leading to a decline in biodiversity in the species and a loss of native indigenous <em>C. sativa</em> varieties [<a href="#B46-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B46-plants-10-00185">46</a>]. The ease of interbreeding within <em>Cannabis</em> has resulted in a highly polymorphic genome, which has led many researchers to classify <em>Cannabis sativa</em> as a monospecific, highly polymorphic species [<a href="#B32-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B32-plants-10-00185">32</a>,<a href="#B47-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B47-plants-10-00185">47</a>,<a href="#B48-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B48-plants-10-00185">48</a>,<a href="#B49-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B49-plants-10-00185">49</a>]. This debate is ongoing and has been reviewed extensively [<a href="#B32-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B32-plants-10-00185">32</a>,<a href="#B34-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34-plants-10-00185">34</a>,<a href="#B45-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B45-plants-10-00185">45</a>]. However, it is not the focus of this review and we will refer to species as presented by the authors when possible.</p></section><section id="sec4-plants-10-00185"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">4. Current Production Practices</h2>
<p><em>Cannabis</em> is a highly adaptable species that can be grown in a variety of conditions, including outdoors in tropical or temperate climates or in controlled environments ranging from rudimentary greenhouse structures to sophisticated controlled environment facilities (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>C,D) [<a href="#B36-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B36-plants-10-00185">36</a>]. The production system of choice is determined based on the end-use of the plant. Plants grown to produce low-value commodities such as oilseed or fiber are typically cultivated exclusively outdoors, where production costs are low. In contrast, plants cultivated for dried flowers for recreational or medicinal use can be cultivated outdoors, in greenhouses, or in indoor production facilities. While production costs for recreational/medicinal products are also lower outdoors, there is a general belief that indoor production facilities produce higher-quality products, which justifies the extra costs for premium flowers [<a href="#B50-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B50-plants-10-00185">50</a>]. However, with the growing trend toward extracts and purified cannabinoids, it is likely that much of the medicinal/recreational production (CBD from hemp, THC from drug-type <em>Cannabis</em>) will be done outdoors to capitalize on these lower production costs. The higher level of oversight offered in controlled environments also allows for easier management of insects and diseases, which is important in order to meet strict government health and safety regulations surrounding the use of chemical control agents, microbial load, and other quality assurance (QA) requirements [<a href="#B51-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B51-plants-10-00185">51</a>,<a href="#B52-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B52-plants-10-00185">52</a>]. These regulations have driven most of the commercial drug-type <em>Cannabis</em> production into greenhouses and indoor facilities for now [<a href="#B53-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B53-plants-10-00185">53</a>].</p>
<p>As with production systems, the approach to plant propagation is influenced by the end-use of the plants. Traditionally, hemp has been cultivated by seed using large-scale, highly mechanized, production practices similar to other grain crops [<a href="#B54-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B54-plants-10-00185">54</a>]. In contrast, drug/recreational <em>Cannabis</em> is generally propagated using clonal methods and treated as a horticultural crop [<a href="#B51-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B51-plants-10-00185">51</a>,<a href="#B52-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B52-plants-10-00185">52</a>,<a href="#B55-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B55-plants-10-00185">55</a>]. This is done to mitigate the high level of phenotypic diversity displayed within seedling populations and to consistently produce high quality, uniform crops that meet consumer preferences and comply with government regulations [<a href="#B56-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B56-plants-10-00185">56</a>]. While this variability also exists in hemp seed, the benefits of clonal propagation and manual planting do not justify the costs for oilseed or fiber [<a href="#B57-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B57-plants-10-00185">57</a>]. However, new regulations surrounding the use of hemp to produce CBD and other non-psychoactive cannabinoids have led some hemp producers to use clonal propagation [<a href="#B18-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18-plants-10-00185">18</a>,<a href="#B54-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B54-plants-10-00185">54</a>,<a href="#B57-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B57-plants-10-00185">57</a>].</p>
<p>Clonal propagation can take many forms, but traditionally, <em>Cannabis</em> has been propagated through stem cuttings. In general, <em>Cannabis</em> is relatively easy to root, and large numbers of plants can be produced from a single mother plant [<a href="#B55-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B55-plants-10-00185">55</a>]. While more expensive than seed, this approach can be efficiently used to mass-produce genetically and phenotypically uniform plants at a commercial scale to produce a more uniform crop. However, this approach requires the maintenance of mother plants in a vegetative state and can occupy 10–15% of the floor space in a commercial operation. The maintenance of mother plants also requires them to remain in a vegetative state. While this is easily accomplished for most genotypes, it presents challenges for day-neutral genotypes as they do not respond to photoperiod [<a href="#B29-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-plants-10-00185">29</a>]. Perhaps of greatest importance, though, is that mother plants are susceptible to insects, pathogens, and viruses and can transmit these biotic factors to their cuttings and lead to problems during production. This is of importance in <em>Cannabis</em> as there are currently very few control options registered for the crop and there is a strong consumer preference for no pesticide use [<a href="#B18-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18-plants-10-00185">18</a>,<a href="#B51-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B51-plants-10-00185">51</a>,<a href="#B52-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B52-plants-10-00185">52</a>].</p></section><section id="sec5-plants-10-00185"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">5. Micropropagation of <em>C. sativa</em>
</h2>
<p>An alternative approach to clonal propagation that addresses many of the challenges of conventional <em>C. sativa</em> propagation is the use of micropropagation, which uses plant tissue culture to mass-propagate plants in a highly controlled environment using aseptic techniques. In micropropagation, plants are cultivated in culture vessels, typically in a multi-tier culture room or even in stackable vessels outfitted with light emitting diode (LED) lighting [<a href="#B58-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B58-plants-10-00185">58</a>] (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>). This allows large numbers of plants to be maintained in a very small space, thereby reducing the amount of floor space required to maintain mother plants. This is particularly attractive for producers that want to maintain a large genetic library but do not want to dedicate the amount of floor space that would be required otherwise. Tissue culture techniques also offer a variety of approaches that may help in maintaining day-neutral genotypes and for long-term genetic preservation. Most importantly, due to the sterile nature of plant tissue culture, it can be used to produce insect-/pathogen-/virus-free propagules to reduce biotic pressures.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-10-00185-f003"><h3 class="obj_head">Figure 3.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7835777_plants-10-00185-g003.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fc6c/7835777/10191e6d2e36/plants-10-00185-g003.jpg" loading="lazy" height="355" width="775" alt="Figure 3"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-10-00185-f003/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Healthy <em>C. sativa</em> explants growing in We-V boxes (<strong>A</strong>). Callus cultures growing in glass culture vessels under LED lighting in a controlled environment growth chamber (<strong>B</strong>) and high-density stackable culture vessels (We-V) with individually programmable LED lighting (<strong>C</strong>) demonstrate the variety and density with which <em>C. sativa</em> can be cultured under In Vitro conditions.</p></figcaption></figure><p>The use of plant tissue culture for the propagation of disease-free plants has provided the foundation for clean plant programs in various crops since the late 1900s [<a href="#B59-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B59-plants-10-00185">59</a>,<a href="#B60-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B60-plants-10-00185">60</a>,<a href="#B61-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B61-plants-10-00185">61</a>]. In some cases, certified disease-free plants produced through tissue culture are planted directly in the field for production, while in other cases, they are used as clean material that is further propagated through other means in highly sanitary conditions and tested for important diseases before being used for commercial production [<a href="#B62-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B62-plants-10-00185">62</a>]. The latter model provides most of the benefits of micropropagation while reducing costs. This approach has been successful in the seed potato industry for developing a disease eradication system [<a href="#B59-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B59-plants-10-00185">59</a>]. In the case of <em>Cannabis</em>, either approach could be taken, and the decision would need to be based on a careful analysis of the costs and benefits by the producer, which will include many factors such as the efficiency of micropropagation, labor costs, the value of additional floor space, risk assessment, and other factors.</p>
<p>The principal challenge in developing effective micropropagation methods is species and genotype specificity, resulting in many variations at each stage of micropropagation. Micropropagation is often broken down into five stages, where each stage needs to be optimized to establish a fully developed micropropagation method (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>) [<a href="#B63-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B63-plants-10-00185">63</a>,<a href="#B64-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64-plants-10-00185">64</a>]. These include Stage 0: Selection/maintenance of parent plant material; Stage 1: Initiation of cultures; Stage 2: Multiplication of shoots/embryos; Stage 3: Shoot elongation and rooting; Stage 4: Acclimatization (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>). While the selection and maintenance of ex vitro stock plants are often ignored, the importance of stock plant health for the subsequent success of the cultures can have a significant impact on further results. Provided that the stock plants from Stage 0 are in good condition, the explants generally respond well to surface disinfection and produce an initial flush of growth during Stage 1. This initial flush of growth is often followed by a more sporadic growth pattern until the explants acclimatize to In Vitro conditions. It is in Stage 2, after plants acclimatize to In Vitro growth, where the largest benefit of micropropagation becomes apparent: the exponential multiplication of plants. Many horticultural crops are maintained for extended periods of time in Stage 2 and continuously sub-cultured for commercial-scale plant production. To illustrate the capability for rapid plant production, an In Vitro protocol using Stage 2 plants with a reasonable multiplication rate of 10 would produce one million plants after only six subcultures (10<sup>6</sup>). When a sufficient quantity of plants has been produced in Stage 2, they are then transferred to Stage 3 to elongate and develop roots, or alternatively, they are transferred directly from their In Vitro environment to an indoor growth facility/greenhouse to acclimatize, thereby combining Stages 3 and 4 (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>). Combining these stages is often preferred for commercial applications as it reduces the number of steps In Vitro, thereby saving time and labor costs [<a href="#B65-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B65-plants-10-00185">65</a>].</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-10-00185-f004"><h3 class="obj_head">Figure 4.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7835777_plants-10-00185-g004.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fc6c/7835777/b22aa7e023c7/plants-10-00185-g004.jpg" loading="lazy" height="411" width="744" alt="Figure 4"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-10-00185-f004/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>The five-stage micropropagation process for tissue culture. The red arrow is indicative of the 1-3-4 approach (where Stage 2 is skipped). Stage 2 is commonly skipped in <em>C. sativa</em> micropropagation methods due to the plant’s recalcitrance to long-term culture characterized by a slow decline in fitness. Inclusion of Stage 2 allows for repeated subcultures of In Vitro plants (indicated by the circular arrows), therefore facilitating large-scale multiplication or long-term germplasm storage.</p></figcaption></figure><p>The earliest In Vitro studies of <em>Cannabis</em> were conducted in hemp and focused on determining its suitability for In Vitro culture and whether tissue culture would affect the agronomic and chemical characteristics of the plant (<a href="#plants-10-00185-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>) [<a href="#B66-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B66-plants-10-00185">66</a>,<a href="#B67-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B67-plants-10-00185">67</a>,<a href="#B68-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B68-plants-10-00185">68</a>,<a href="#B69-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B69-plants-10-00185">69</a>]. Richez-Dumanois et al. [<a href="#B66-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B66-plants-10-00185">66</a>] showed that hemp could be micropropagated using nodal cuttings and the inclusion of IBA and BAP promoted the growth of shoots from existing meristematic tissues. They also demonstrated the successful acclimatization of In Vitro grown hemp to greenhouse conditions [<a href="#B66-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B66-plants-10-00185">66</a>]. Importantly, their work showed that the In Vitro grown plants’ chemical and physical profiles were similar to their greenhouse-grown counterparts. This finding has been reasserted by contemporary studies on medicinal <em>Cannabis</em> that found micropropagation from nodal cuttings had no significant effect on the cannabinoid contents of the mature flowering plant [<a href="#B69-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B69-plants-10-00185">69</a>].</p>
<section class="tw xbox font-sm" id="plants-10-00185-t001"><h3 class="obj_head">Table 1.</h3>
<div class="caption p"><p>Summary of the published micropropagation studies on <em>C. sativa.</em> The table summarizes studies that rely on shoot multiplication (SM) to increase explant number. SM refers to the proliferation of multiple shoots from an existing meristem, such as axillary or apical nodes and floral meristems. <em>Cannabis</em> type is defined in this table as either psychoactive “drug-type” (<em>Cannabis</em>; tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) &gt; 0.3% in flowering head) or known industrial hemp genotypes “fiber-type” (Hemp; THC &lt; 0.3% in flowering head). A breakdown of the cultivars (CVs) used in the study and the number which responded to the treatment are included for each study. N.S. Not specified is assigned to data that were not specified, instances of “data not shown”, or when data are omitted in the original research article.</p></div>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/plants-10-00185-t001/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<div class="tw-foot p"><div class="fn" id="fn2"><p><sup>a</sup> Molarity values converted from mg/L. <sup>b</sup> Authors reported using a high-cannabidiol (CBD) drug-type <em>C. sativa</em>, % THC not specified.</p></div></div></section><p>As it has been well established that <em>Cannabis</em> can be cultured In Vitro without affecting its biochemical outcomes, contemporary studies have shifted to determining the optimal growth and multiplication conditions for each stage of micropropagation, a task complicated by the numerous factors which must be considered when growing a plant In Vitro (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f005" class="usa-link">Figure 5</a>). Existing <em>Cannabis</em> micropropagation studies have primarily taken to optimizing freshly initiated tissues for shoot proliferation, opting to focus on plant growth regulator (PGR) combinations that result in rapid shoot proliferation (<a href="#plants-10-00185-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>) [<a href="#B69-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B69-plants-10-00185">69</a>,<a href="#B72-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B72-plants-10-00185">72</a>,<a href="#B75-plants-10-00185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B75-plants-10-00185">75</a>]. Once developed, the shoots are rooted on an auxin-rich medium and then transferred back into growth facilities (Stages 3 and 4; <a href="#plants-10-00185-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>). These rapid and high-throughput approaches to <em>C. sativa</em> micropropagation are useful but neglect to study the long-term health and maintenance of the explants in culture, evidenced by the relatively few studies reporting results from Stage 2 (<a href="#plants-10-00185-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a> and <a href="#plants-10-00185-t002" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>). The result of this is that explants are not fully acclimatized to In Vitro conditions, and consequentially, the protocols are only optimized for Stages 1, 3, and 4 (a 1-3-4 approach) rather than for the long-term conservation and multiplication of germplasm in Stage 2 (<a href="#plants-10-00185-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a> and <a href="#plants-10-00185-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-10-00185-f005"><h3 class="obj_head">Figure 5.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7835777_plants-10-00185-g005.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fc6c/7835777/9f91f2e29d50/plants-10-00185-g005.jpg" loading="lazy" height="393" width="742" alt="Figure 5"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-10-00185-f005/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Tissue culture of healthy explants relies on the careful optimization of multiple factors. Center image: Freshly subcultured vegetative explant of <em>C. sativa</em>.</p></figcaption></figure><section class="tw xbox font-sm" id="plants-10-00185-t002"><h3 class="obj_head">Table 2.</h3>
<div class="caption p"><p>Summary of the published regeneration studies on <em>C. sativa</em>. The table summarizes studies that rely on shoot regeneration for the production of <em>C. sativa</em> explants. Shoot regeneration refers to the formation of de novo shoots from non-meristematic tissues such as leaves, stems, or cotyledons. This includes direct and indirect organogenesis and somatic embryogenesis from callus or suspension cultures. <em>Cannabis</em> type is defined in this table as either psychoactive “drug-type” (<em>Cannabis</em>; THC &gt; 0.3% in flowering head) or known industrial hemp genotypes “fiber-type” (Hemp; THC &lt; 0.3% in flowering head). A breakdown of the cultivars (CVs) used in the study and the number which responded to the treatment are included for each study. N.S. Not specified is assigned to data that were not specified, instances of “data not shown”, or when data are omitted in the original research article.</p></div>

<strong>Shoot regeneration:</strong><br>MS + 0.5 μM TDZ</td>
<td align="center" valign="top" style="border-bottom:solid thin" rowspan="1" colspan="1">
<strong>Shoot regeneration:</strong><br>Not achieved</td>
